[1x] Verify Redis is running and listening on the expected host:port. Check firewall rules and security groups.
[1x] All cache operations fail immediately. Application falls back to origin (database), causing load surge.
[1x] Connection dropped mid-operation due to a network blip, server restart, or TCP reset.
[1x] Unstable network path, Redis server restarted, or an intermediate proxy closed the connection.
[1x] Enable automatic reconnection in the client. Use connection health checks and retry with backoff.
[1x] In-flight commands are lost. Clients see transient errors until reconnection succeeds.
[1x] Connection or operation exceeded the configured timeout. Redis or the network is too slow to respond.
[1x] Redis overloaded, slow network, or timeout configured too aggressively for the workload.
[1x] Tune timeout values. Investigate Redis slowlog for long-running commands. Consider connection pooling.
[1x] Commands fail with timeout errors. Application latency spikes as it waits then falls back to origin.
[1x] DNS resolution failed for the Redis hostname. The client cannot resolve the server address.
[1x] DNS misconfiguration, hostname typo, or DNS infrastructure failure.
[1x] Verify hostname spelling and DNS configuration. Use IP addresses as fallback. Monitor DNS health.
[1x] Complete cache unavailability. All operations fail at the DNS resolution stage.
[1x] Client closed connection before a response arrived. Usually caused by aggressive client-side timeouts.
[1x] Client timeout too short for the operation, or the client was shut down mid-request.
[1x] Align client timeout with expected operation duration. Use connection keep-alive settings.
[1x] Operations may complete on Redis but the client never sees the response. Risk of inconsistent state.
[1x] NOAUTH Authentication required
[1x] Password not sent but Redis requires authentication. Client connected without AUTH.
[1x] Redis configured with requirepass but client not configured with credentials.
[1x] Configure client with the correct Redis password. Use ACL-based authentication for fine-grained access.
[1x] All commands fail after connection. Redis rejects every operation until AUTH is successful.
[1x] Wrong password, or AUTH sent when not required by the server.
[1x] Credential mismatch after rotation, copy-paste error, or AUTH sent to a server without requirepass.
[1x] Verify credentials match. Implement credential rotation with zero-downtime swap. Check ACL configuration.
[1x] Client is connected but cannot execute any commands. Equivalent to full cache outage.
[1x] Max reconnection attempts reached
[1x] Client gave up retrying after too many consecutive connection failures.
[1x] Extended Redis outage, persistent network issue, or reconnection backoff/limit too aggressive.
[1x] Increase max retries or use exponential backoff with jitter. Implement a circuit breaker to avoid thundering herd on recovery.
[1x] Application permanently loses cache access until process restart or manual intervention.
[1x] Connection lost \u2013 Attempting reconnect
[1x] Client lost connection and is in the process of retrying. Transient state during recovery.
[1x] Network blip, Redis restart, or keepalive timeout expired.
[1x] Monitor reconnection duration. If frequent, investigate underlying network stability or Redis health.
[1x] Cache operations fail during reconnection window. Short-lived if Redis is healthy.
[1x] WRONGTYPE Operation against a key holding the wrong kind of value
[1x] Calling a command designed for one data type on a key that holds a different type (e.g. LPUSH on a String key).
[1x] Application logic bug, key naming collision, or schema migration left stale keys of the wrong type.
[1x] Use consistent key naming conventions with type prefixes. Add TYPE checks before operations. Clean up stale keys.
[1x] The specific command fails. If unhandled, can cascade into application errors for the affected feature.
[1x] ERR wrong number of arguments
[1x] Missing or extra arguments passed to a Redis command.
[1x] Application code bug, client library version mismatch, or incorrect command wrapper.
[1x] Fix the command invocation. Validate arguments before sending. Keep client library up to date.
[1x] Command is rejected. Feature relying on that command fails until code is fixed.
[1x] ERR value is not an integer or out of range
[1x] Passing a float or string to INCR, EXPIRE, or similar commands that require integer arguments.
[1x] Type coercion bug in application code, or stored value corrupted from a different code path.
[1x] Validate input types before sending commands. Use defensive parsing for values read from external sources.
[1x] Atomic increment/decrement operations fail. Rate limiters, counters, or TTL management breaks.
[1x] Malformed command sent to the server. Redis cannot parse the command string.
[1x] String interpolation bug, unsupported command on this Redis version, or protocol-level corruption.
[1x] Use a well-tested client library. Avoid raw command string construction. Validate Redis version compatibility.
[1x] Command is rejected with no retry possible. Must be fixed in code.
[1x] Key does not exist. Usually safe but worth handling explicitly to avoid unexpected nil returns.
[1x] Key expired via TTL, was evicted under memory pressure, or was never set.
[1x] Handle nil returns gracefully in application code. Use EXISTS check where needed. Monitor key eviction rates.
[1x] Usually benign; becomes problematic if application treats missing keys as errors instead of cache misses.
[1x] EXECABORT Transaction discarded
[1x] A command failed inside a MULTI block, aborting the entire transaction.
[1x] One command in the transaction has a syntax or type error. Redis rejects the entire batch.
[1x] Validate all commands before MULTI/EXEC. Use WATCH for optimistic locking. Handle EXECABORT with retry logic.
[1x] Entire atomic transaction is rolled back. Multi-step cache operations left in an inconsistent state.
[1x] EXEC called outside of a MULTI transaction block. Protocol-level logic error.
[1x] State machine bug in transaction handling, or connection was reset mid-transaction.
[1x] Ensure MULTI/EXEC pairs are atomic in your code. Handle connection reset by restarting the transaction.
[1x] Command rejected. If connection dropped mid-MULTI, partial commands may have been queued and lost.
[1x] WATCH key was modified
[1x] Optimistic locking failed \u2014 the watched key was changed by another client between WATCH and EXEC.
[1x] High write contention on the watched key. Multiple clients competing to update the same data.
[1x] Implement retry logic for WATCH/MULTI/EXEC. Consider using Lua scripts for atomic operations instead.
[1x] Transaction is silently discarded (EXEC returns nil). Must be detected and retried by application code.
[1x] OOM command not allowed when used memory > maxmemory
[1x] Redis hit the maxmemory limit with noeviction policy set. New writes are rejected.
[1x] Insufficient memory allocation, no eviction policy configured, or unexpected data growth.
[1x] Set an appropriate maxmemory-policy (allkeys-lru or volatile-lru). Increase maxmemory or add shards.
[1x] All write commands fail. Read commands still work. Application cannot store new data in cache.
[1x] Used memory RSS exceeds maxmemory
[1x] OS-level memory fragmentation. Actual RSS is higher than the configured maxmemory limit.
[1x] Memory fragmentation from frequent allocation/deallocation patterns. Common with variable-size values.
[1x] Run MEMORY PURGE. Consider a rolling restart. Use jemalloc allocator. Monitor mem_fragmentation_ratio.
[1x] Risk of OS OOM killer terminating Redis. Performance degrades as OS starts swapping.
[1x] Memory fragmentation is high. Redis is using significantly more RSS than the logical dataset size.
[1x] Frequent key deletion and creation of different sizes. Allocator cannot efficiently reuse freed memory.
[1x] Run MEMORY PURGE or schedule a rolling restart. Normalize value sizes where possible.
[1x] Wasted memory reduces effective cache capacity. May trigger OOM before maxmemory is logically full.
[1x] maxmemory-policy evictions firing unexpectedly
[1x] Eviction policy (LRU/LFU/volatile-*) is silently deleting keys to stay under maxmemory.
[1x] Cache size too small for the working set. Or volatile-* policy evicting keys with TTL that should be retained.
[1x] Increase maxmemory. Review eviction policy. Monitor evicted_keys metric. Consider splitting hot and cold data.
[1x] Cache hit rate drops as frequently-accessed keys are evicted. Backend load increases.
[1x] WARNING: 32 bit instance detected
[1x] Running 32-bit Redis which has a hard 4 GB memory ceiling.
[1x] Legacy installation or container image using 32-bit Redis build.
[1x] Migrate to 64-bit Redis. All modern deployments should use 64-bit builds.
[1x] Cache capacity capped at 4 GB regardless of available system memory. Will hit OOM quickly under load.
[1x] Background RDB save failed due to disk full, permission error, or I/O issue.
[1x] Insufficient disk space, wrong file permissions, or disk hardware failure.
[1x] Check disk space and permissions. Monitor disk usage. Configure RDB save path on a reliable volume.
[1x] No point-in-time snapshot. Data at risk of loss on crash. Redis may block writes if stop-writes-on-bgsave-error is yes.
[1x] AOF compaction/rewrite failed. Check disk space and permissions.
[1x] Disk full during rewrite, permission error, or background process killed.
[1x] Free disk space. Check permissions on AOF directory. Monitor AOF file size growth.
[1x] AOF file grows unbounded without compaction. Restart recovery time increases. Disk may fill up.
[1x] Can't save in background: fork: Cannot allocate memory
[1x] OS lacks free memory to fork a child process for the RDB snapshot.
[1x] Redis uses fork() for BGSAVE. The OS needs enough free memory for copy-on-write pages. overcommit_memory not set.
[1x] Set vm.overcommit_memory = 1. Ensure system has headroom for fork(). Consider using AOF-only persistence.
[1x] No RDB snapshots can be created. Data durability is compromised. May also affect replication initial sync.
[1x] WARNING overcommit_memory is set to 0
[1x] OS may kill child processes during RDB or AOF fork operations. Risk of failed snapshots.
[1x] Linux kernel setting vm.overcommit_memory defaults to 0 (heuristic), which may deny fork for large datasets.
[1x] Set vm.overcommit_memory = 1 in sysctl. This is a standard Redis production hardening step.
[1x] BGSAVE and BGREWRITEAOF may fail intermittently. Data persistence unreliable.
[1x] AOF enabled but no file opened
[1x] AOF file path is misconfigured or a disk issue is preventing file creation.
[1x] Non-existent directory in appendfilename config, or disk mount point failed.
[1x] Verify appendfilename and dir configuration. Ensure the target directory exists and is writable.
[1x] AOF is enabled but not recording. Complete data loss on crash, despite user believing persistence is active.
[1x] Saving the final RDB snapshot before exiting \u2014 failed
[1x] Shutdown-time RDB snapshot could not be written. Data since last successful snapshot is lost.
[1x] Disk full or permission error at shutdown time. Same root cause as BGSAVE failures.
[1x] Ensure disk space is available. Monitor BGSAVE success. Use AOF as a safety net.
[1x] Data loss on restart. Magnitude depends on time since last successful persistence write.
[1x] READONLY You can't write against a read only replica
[1x] Write command sent to a replica node. Must target the master.
[1x] Client routing error, failover not detected, or read/write splitting misconfigured.
[1x] Route write commands to the master. Use client-side write routing or a proxy like Redis Sentinel.
[1x] Write operations fail. If writes are critical-path, the feature is fully broken until routing is fixed.
[1x] Partial resynchronization not possible
[1x] Replication backlog too small. A full resync was triggered instead of an incremental one.
[1x] repl-backlog-size too small for the write throughput, or replica was disconnected too long.
[1x] Increase repl-backlog-size. Monitor replica offset drift. Reduce disconnect duration.
[1x] Full resync transfers the entire dataset, consuming bandwidth and CPU. Master may slow down during transfer.
[1x] MISCONF Redis is configured to save RDB snapshots but cannot persist
[1x] RDB persistence required but failing. Redis blocks writes to prevent data loss.
[1x] BGSAVE error combined with stop-writes-on-bgsave-error = yes (the default).
[1x] Fix the underlying BGSAVE failure (disk space, permissions). Or set stop-writes-on-bgsave-error no (accepting risk).
[1x] All write operations rejected. Equivalent to a full write outage until persistence is restored.
[1x] Replica is lagging behind master
[1x] Network or disk bottleneck is causing replication lag on the replica.
[1x] High write volume on master, slow replica disk, network congestion, or replica overloaded with reads.
[1x] Monitor master_repl_offset vs replica offset. Reduce replica read load. Upgrade replica hardware.
[1x] Master aborted replication with an error
[1x] Master rejected the replica during the handshake phase.
[1x] Version incompatibility, auth mismatch, or master under extreme memory pressure during BGSAVE for initial sync.
[1x] Check Redis version compatibility. Verify replica auth credentials. Ensure master has headroom for fork.
[1x] Replica cannot sync. No redundancy until replication is re-established. Failover capability lost.
[1x] MOVED <slot> <host>:<port>
[1x] Key belongs to a different node. Client must redirect the request.
[1x] Client not using cluster-aware mode, or slot mapping is stale after a rebalance.
[1x] Use a cluster-aware client library. Enable automatic redirection. Refresh slot mapping on MOVED errors.
[1x] Extra round-trip latency per MOVED redirect. Clients without cluster support fail entirely.
[1x] ASK <slot> <host>:<port>
[1x] Key is migrating to another node. Send ASKING before retrying on the target node.
[1x] Slot migration in progress during a cluster rebalance operation.
[1x] Handle ASK redirects in the client. Wait for migration to complete. Avoid large multi-key operations during rebalancing.
[1x] Slight latency increase during migration. Transparent if client handles ASK correctly.
[1x] CLUSTERDOWN The cluster is down
[1x] Not enough master nodes are reachable to maintain quorum.
[1x] Multiple master failures, network partition isolating masters, or cluster not properly initialized.
[1x] Ensure sufficient master+replica redundancy. Monitor cluster health. Use Redis Sentinel or managed Redis with auto-failover.
[1x] Complete cache cluster outage. All operations fail until quorum is restored.
[1x] CROSSSLOT Keys in request don't hash to the same slot
[1x] Multi-key operation spans different hash slots. Not allowed in cluster mode.
[1x] Application uses MGET/MSET or Lua scripts with keys that hash to different slots.
[1x] Use hash tags ({prefix}) to colocate related keys in the same slot. Redesign multi-key operations.
[1x] Multi-key atomic operations fail. Must be redesigned for cluster compatibility.
[1x] TRYAGAIN Multiple keys request during rehashing
[1x] Slot migration is in progress. Retry the request shortly.
[1x] Multi-key command hit while the slot is being migrated between nodes.
[1x] Implement retry with short backoff. Avoid large multi-key operations during rebalancing.
[1x] Transient failures during cluster rebalancing. Self-resolves once migration completes.
[1x] NOSCRIPT No matching script
[1x] Script not loaded on this server. Use EVALSHA after SCRIPT LOAD.
[1x] Redis restarted and lost cached scripts, or client is using EVALSHA without a SCRIPT LOAD fallback.
[1x] Implement EVALSHA with EVAL fallback pattern. Re-load scripts on connection establishment.
[1x] Lua-script-dependent operations fail. Features using atomic Lua scripts are broken until scripts are reloaded.
[1x] ERR Error running script
[1x] A runtime error occurred inside the Lua script.
[1x] Bug in Lua code, nil dereference, type mismatch, or exceeding script limits.
[1x] Debug the Lua script locally. Add error handling within the script. Test with representative data.
[1x] The atomic operation fails. Partial state changes inside the script are rolled back by Redis.
[1x] A long-running Lua script is blocking the server. Use SCRIPT KILL to terminate.
[1x] Lua script with O(n) operations on large datasets, infinite loops, or missing iteration limits.
[1x] Set lua-time-limit. Break large operations into smaller scripts. Use SCRIPT KILL or SHUTDOWN NOSAVE in emergencies.
[1x] Entire Redis instance is blocked. All clients see timeouts. Equivalent to a full outage.
[1x] Script attempted to access a non local key
[1x] Cluster mode: Lua script accessed keys on different hash slots.
[1x] Script KEYS argument contains keys from multiple slots. Not allowed in cluster mode.
[1x] Use hash tags to colocate keys. Redesign scripts to operate on keys within a single slot.
[1x] Script fails with error. Atomic operation cannot be performed across slots.
[1x] Sentinel detected master as subjectively or objectively down.
[1x] Master crashed, network partition, or master too slow to respond to PING within sentinel down-after-milliseconds.
[1x] Tune down-after-milliseconds. Ensure Redis health checks are responsive. Monitor sentinel logs.
[1x] Triggers failover process. Writes may be rejected during failover window (typically 5-30 seconds).
[1x] Failover in progress
[1x] A replica is being promoted to master by Sentinel.
[1x] Master failure detected and quorum agreed on failover. Normal HA behavior.
[1x] Monitor failover duration. Ensure clients re-discover the new master via Sentinel. Use connection retry logic.
[1x] Brief write outage during promotion (5-30 seconds). Reads from replicas may continue.
[1x] Not enough sentinels
[1x] Quorum not met. Failover cannot proceed without enough Sentinel votes.
[1x] Too few Sentinel instances running, or network partition isolating Sentinels from each other.
[1x] Deploy at least 3 Sentinel instances across failure domains. Monitor Sentinel process health.
[1x] Automatic failover is disabled. If master fails, manual intervention is required for recovery.
[1x] Sentinel can't connect to master
[1x] Network partition or master crash detected by Sentinel.
[1x] Firewall rules changed, master process died, or network path between Sentinel and master is broken.
[1x] Verify network connectivity between Sentinel and master. Check firewall rules. Review master process health.
[1x] Sentinel cannot monitor master health. If master is actually down, failover will be delayed or blocked.
[1x] Redis returned an error response to a client command.
[1x] Command-level error: wrong arguments, type mismatch, permission denied, or server-side rejection.
[1x] Inspect the error message for the specific Redis error code. Fix the offending command.
[1x] Individual command fails. Impact depends on whether the error is caught and handled by the application.
[1x] Command was aborted because the connection was lost mid-flight.
[1x] Network failure, Redis restart, or client-side timeout killed the connection while a command was pending.
[1x] Implement retry logic with idempotency. Use connection pooling with health checks.
[1x] Pending commands are lost. Application must detect and retry or fail gracefully.
[1x] MaxRetriesPerRequestError
[1x] The client library gave up retrying a failed command (e.g. ioredis maxRetriesPerRequest).
[1x] Sustained Redis unavailability exceeding the configured retry budget.
[1x] Increase maxRetriesPerRequest or implement application-level fallback. Add circuit breaker to prevent retry storms.
[1x] Application code receives unrecoverable error. Cache-dependent features fail.

[1x] Container or VM exceeds its CPU quota/limit. Kernel throttles the process, adding latency to every syscall and computation during the burst period.
[1x] P99 latency spikes; users experience slow API responses even though the app itself is healthy.
[1x] All worker threads are busy handling in-flight requests. New requests queue up in the OS accept queue or the application queue until a thread is free.
[1x] Synchronous callers time out waiting for a thread. Cascading 503s upstream.
[1x] Thread Pool Saturation
[1x] Container memory usage exceeds its limit. The Linux OOM killer terminates the process immediately with no warning. All in-flight requests are lost.
[1x] Sudden 502/503 burst. Requests mid-execution are dropped with no error response to the client.
[1x] OOM Kill (OOMKilled)
[1x] ) block all application threads.',
[1x] Periodic latency spikes correlating with GC cycles. P99 degrades while P50 stays normal.
[1x] Heap Pressure / GC Storm
[1x] Too many threads/goroutines competing for CPU cores. OS spends more time context-switching than doing useful work. Usually caused by massively over-provisioned thread pools.
[1x] CPU utilization looks high but throughput is low. Latency increases non-linearly with concurrency.
[1x] Context Switch Storm
[1x] High-rate API server opens many short-lived outbound connections (to DB, cache, downstream APIs) and exhausts the 32k\u201360k ephemeral port range before TIME_WAIT sockets expire.
[1x] New outbound connections fail with EADDRNOTAVAIL. API calls to downstream services start failing intermittently.
[1x] Ephemeral Port Exhaustion
[1x] A single code path (JSON serialization, regex, cryptographic operation) consuming disproportionate CPU on every request, limiting overall throughput.
[1x] Throughput cap lower than expected even at moderate load. Adding more instances required.
[1x] Hot Path CPU Bottleneck
[1x] Application accumulates memory over time without releasing it. Not visible in first hours. Eventually triggers OOMKill or degraded GC. Often caused by unclosed connections or unbounded caches.
[1x] Memory grows monotonically per instance over hours/days; pods restart on a schedule, masking the root cause.
[1x] Memory Leak (Slow Ramp)
[1x] A synchronous blocking call (file read, DNS lookup, slow DB) made inside an async event loop (Node.js, asyncio). Blocks the entire event loop, stalling all concurrent requests.
[1x] One slow operation freezes all concurrent requests. Throughput collapses to near zero during the block.
[1x] Blocking I/O on Async Loop
[1x] During rolling deploy, the instance's shutdown hook doesn't drain in-flight requests within the termination grace period. Kubernetes SIGKILL fires, dropping active requests.
[1x] Brief burst of 500 errors on every deploy. Worse with long-running synchronous calls.
[1x] Graceful Shutdown Timeout
[1x] App returns non-200 on readiness probe during startup or an internal dependency check. Kubernetes removes the pod from service before it fails completely.
[1x] Pod cycles out of rotation; traffic re-distributed to remaining healthy pods, increasing their load.
[1x] Readiness Probe Failure
[1x] Liveness probe times out during GC pause or high CPU, causing Kubernetes to restart a healthy pod. Probe timeout threshold set too low.
[1x] Unnecessary restarts under load; transient 503s. Can cause a cascading restart loop under sustained high traffic.
[1x] Liveness Probe False Positive
[1x] The underlying host is running slower than expected because of hypervisor contention, noisy-neighbour activity, or degraded node performance. Latency rises even though the app itself is not saturating CPU.
[1x] Latency spikes appear without matching application-level CPU saturation. Drain or replace the affected host if the slowdown persists.
[1x] Underlying Host Slowdown
[1x] Large request payload buffered entirely in memory before processing. With high concurrency, aggregate buffer size causes memory pressure and slows GC.
[1x] Memory usage spikes with request size \xd7 concurrency. Switch to streaming body parsing.
[1x] Request Body Buffer Overflow
[1x] A thread repeatedly polls a condition in a tight loop without sleeping, consuming 100% of a CPU core while waiting. Often introduced accidentally in async polling logic or broken mutex implementations.
[1x] One thread monopolises a CPU core, starving other request handlers. Visible as one core pegged at 100% while others are idle.
[1x] Spin Lock / Busy Wait
[1x] The kernel's TCP accept queue (backlog) fills up because the application is not calling accept() fast enough during a traffic spike. New incoming SYN packets are silently dropped.
[1x] Clients see connection timeouts even though the server is running. No error is logged on the server side \u2014 requires netstat/ss monitoring to detect.
[1x] TCP Accept Queue Overflow
[1x] Request crashes with an unrecoverable error. In JVM, the exception may be caught at a wrong layer, masking the root cause from logs.
[1x] Stack Overflow / Deep Recursion
[1x] All outbound and inbound connections start failing. Application appears to hang. Requires immediate process restart and ulimit increase.
[1x] File Descriptor Exhaustion
[1x] A poorly written regular expression with exponential backtracking applied to user-supplied input. A crafted string causes the regex engine to spin for seconds or minutes on a single request, pegging one CPU core.
[1x] One malicious or unexpected request can freeze a thread for minutes, reducing concurrency by 1 thread for its duration. Easily weaponised for DoS.
[1x] Regex ReDoS (Catastrophic Backtracking)
[1x] Load balancer's keep-alive timeout is longer than the application server's. The app closes the connection while the LB thinks it is still valid and tries to reuse it for the next request.
[1x] Intermittent 502s that are hard to reproduce. Happens precisely when a connection's age crosses the app-side timeout. Fix: set LB timeout slightly shorter than app timeout.
[1x] Keep-Alive Misconfig Between LB and App
[1x] Serialization of large or deeply nested response objects consumes a disproportionate amount of CPU relative to actual business logic. Common when returning large lists without pagination.
[1x] Serialization CPU Spike (JSON/Protobuf)
[1x] Worker acquires a lock (DB row, Redis key, mutex) and never releases it due to an unhandled exception or missing finally block. The job neither succeeds nor fails.
[1x] Queue depth grows while hung workers hold locks. Other workers stall waiting. Invisible unless you monitor lock age and job duration.
[1x] A malformed or unexpected message causes the worker to crash on every processing attempt. The message is re-queued each time, consuming a worker and CPU indefinitely.
[1x] Worker crashes in a tight loop; error rate spikes; the bad message blocks queue progress behind it.
[1x] Worker killed by OOM or fatal exception while processing a job. Job may be re-queued (at-least-once delivery) causing duplicate processing, or dropped (at-most-once), causing data loss.
[1x] Silent data inconsistency or duplicate side effects. Requires idempotency on all job handlers.
[1x] Worker Crash / OOMKill Mid-Job
[1x] Job arrival rate exceeds worker processing rate. Queue depth (consumer lag) grows continuously. Caused by insufficient worker replicas, slow processing, or a sudden traffic spike.
[1x] Background tasks delayed minutes to hours. Time-sensitive operations (email, notifications, webhooks) violate SLA.
[1x] Queue Lag / Consumer Lag
[1x] Job exceeds max execution time and is killed by the scheduler, but cleanup code (releasing locks, rolling back DB state, completing partial writes) is not run.
[1x] Orphaned database locks, incomplete file uploads, or half-applied mutations left in the system.
[1x] Job Timeout Without Cleanup
[1x] Job fails repeatedly beyond the configured max retry count and moves to the Dead Letter Queue (DLQ) / dead letter topic. No automatic recovery \u2014 requires manual intervention.
[1x] Work items silently disappear from the main queue. DLQ fills up unnoticed if monitoring is absent.
[1x] Retry Exhaustion / Dead Letter
[1x] Network failure between queue broker and worker causes a message to be redelivered after it was already processed. Worker is not idempotent, so the action runs twice.
[1x] Double charges, duplicate emails, double-booked resources, or duplicate DB rows.
[1x] Duplicate Execution (Non-Idempotent)
[1x] Scheduled job takes longer than its interval. Next run is triggered before the previous one completes. Two instances run concurrently, causing race conditions on shared state.
[1x] Data corruption or double-processing in batch aggregations, report generation, or cleanup jobs.
[1x] Cron Overlap / Double Trigger
[1x] High-priority or large jobs monopolise all worker threads. Low-priority jobs never get scheduled. Common with a single shared worker pool and no job-type partitioning.
[1x] Low-priority queues grow indefinitely; certain job types never complete.
[1x] SQS/RabbitMQ visibility timeout expires before the worker finishes processing. The message becomes visible again and is consumed by another worker while still being processed.
[1x] Non-idempotent concurrent execution of the same job. Extend visibility timeout or heartbeat to avoid.
[1x] Message Visibility Timeout Race
[1x] Multiple scheduler instances across nodes with unsynchronised clocks trigger the same cron job at different times. NTP drift causes near-simultaneous duplicate executions.
[1x] Intermittent duplicate job runs that are hard to reproduce. Mitigated by distributed lock on scheduler.
[1x] Scheduler Clock Skew
[1x] Long-running job stops sending heartbeat signals to the orchestrator (Celery, Temporal, Sidekiq). Orchestrator assumes the worker is dead and re-schedules the job on another node.
[1x] Wasted compute on partially completed work. Risk of two workers finishing the same job.
[1x] Missing Heartbeat / Progress Stall
[1x] Worker child process terminates but the parent doesn't call wait(), leaving it as a zombie. Over time, zombie accumulation fills the process table. No new workers can be forked.
[1x] Worker pool silently shrinks. Queue lag increases gradually until the host is rebooted or the supervisor is restarted.
[1x] Worker Process Zombie
[1x] A low-priority job holds a shared resource (DB lock, mutex, semaphore) that a high-priority job needs. The high-priority job blocks waiting, effectively running at the priority of the low-priority holder.
[1x] Urgent jobs stuck behind low-priority work. Entire job pipeline stalls on a seemingly unrelated low-priority task.
[1x] Priority Inversion in Queue
[1x] Worker concurrency set too high relative to available DB connections or memory. Each worker opens its own DB connection pool; with 20 workers \xd7 10 connections each, the DB is flooded with 200 connections.
[1x] Database connection exhaustion. All workers fail simultaneously. Reducing worker count or using a connection proxy (PgBouncer) is required.
[1x] Concurrency Limit Misconfiguration
[1x] Job completes and stores its result in a temporary store (Redis, Celery result backend) with a TTL. The consumer retrieves the result after the TTL has expired \u2014 the result is gone.
[1x] Consumer receives a missing result error and must re-trigger the job, causing duplicate compute and delayed response to callers.
[1x] Job Result Expiry Before Consumption
[1x] Worker fetches a batch of messages and acknowledges them before fully processing. A crash after ack but before completion silently drops all messages in that batch with no redelivery.
[1x] Silent data loss. No error is raised; queue depth drops normally. Requires post-processing audit to detect missing records.
[1x] Batch Acknowledge Without Processing
[1x] New worker pods take 30\u2013120 seconds to start (JVM warmup, model load, dependency init). During scale-out, new workers are not processing jobs during this window, creating a temporary capacity gap.
[1x] Queue lag spikes during scale events before new workers are ready. Use pre-initialised warm pools or smaller worker startup payloads.
[1x] Cold Start Latency on Worker Scale-Out
[1x] Cloud provider reclaims a spot/preemptible instance mid-batch with a 2-minute warning. If the workload isn't checkpoint-aware, all progress since the last checkpoint is lost.
[1x] Entire batch must restart from last checkpoint. Without checkpointing, restarts from zero \u2014 wasting hours of compute.
[1x] Spot / Preemption Kill
[1x] One stage in a multi-step pipeline (extract -> transform -> load) fails. Downstream stages either proceed on bad data or are blocked, depending on pipeline design.
[1x] Downstream stages produce incorrect results silently, or the entire pipeline stalls and misses its batch window.
[1x] Pipeline Stage Failure
[1x] Batch job attempts to load an entire dataset into memory. Works fine in test but fails in production with larger data. Instance killed by OOM before the job completes.
[1x] Batch job always fails at the same scale threshold. Requires streaming/chunked processing refactor.
[1x] Out-of-Memory on Large Dataset
[1x] Input data partitioned unevenly \u2014 some partitions have 100\xd7 more records than others. Workers assigned to large partitions take much longer, creating a long tail that extends total batch time.
[1x] Batch window missed. Idle workers waiting for the one slow worker to finish before the job completes.
[1x] Data Skew / Hot Partition
[1x] Batch job reads/writes large files to local disk, saturating disk throughput. All other I/O on the node slows. Common during shuffle phases in Spark/MapReduce jobs.
[1x] I/O wait dominates CPU time. Actual processing throughput collapses. Other jobs on the same node are degraded.
[1x] Batch job cannot write progress checkpoint to storage (S3, GCS, NFS) due to permission error, disk full, or network issue. Job continues but all progress will be lost on restart.
[1x] If the job is later killed, it restarts from zero instead of from the checkpoint, potentially redoing hours of work.
[1x] Checkpoint Write Failure
[1x] Input data format changed (new column, removed field, type changed) between batch runs. ETL pipeline fails to parse records or silently produces null/default values for missing fields.
[1x] Silent data quality degradation in output. Downstream dashboards or ML models consume bad data without knowing.
[1x] Schema Mismatch on Input
[1x] Batch job takes longer than the allotted time window. Next scheduled run starts before the current one completes, causing pipeline conflicts or missed downstream dependencies.
[1x] Stale data in reports; downstream pipelines waiting on this batch have to skip or fail.
[1x] Batch Window Overrun
[1x] Batch dispatched across nodes with different CPU/memory/disk performance (spot + on-demand mix). Slower nodes extend total completion time and can cause partition-level failures.
[1x] Unpredictable batch completion times. Hard to capacity-plan or SLA-guarantee.
[1x] Worker Node Heterogeneity
[1x] Batch job writes incrementally to the output destination and fails mid-write. Output table/file contains a mix of new and old data, with no atomic commit boundary.
[1x] Downstream consumers read corrupt partial results. Requires output table isolation (write-then-swap pattern).
[1x] Partial Output / Dirty Write
[1x] Batch job fans out to thousands of parallel sub-tasks simultaneously. Each sub-task opens DB/API connections, exhausting the connection pool even if each individual task is fast.
[1x] Database or API rate-limit errors at the sub-task level. Batch throughput capped far below theoretical maximum.
[1x] Fanout Resource Exhaustion
[1x] Batch job starts but the data source (S3 path, database table, Kafka topic) is missing, empty, or inaccessible due to IAM permission change or source deletion. Job either errors immediately or silently produces empty output.
[1x] Empty or failed batch run. Downstream consumers receive stale or zero records. Silent if the job exits with code 0 on empty input without validation.
[1x] Input Source Unavailable
[1x] Large-scale batch job reading from or writing to remote storage (S3, GCS, HDFS) saturates the instance's network interface. Other processes on the same host experience degraded network performance.
[1x] Batch throughput plateaus at the network bandwidth limit, not CPU. Other services sharing the host degrade. Use dedicated instances or network-optimised instance types.
[1x] Network Bandwidth Saturation
[1x] Spark/Flink shuffle stage spills intermediate data to local disk because it exceeds available memory. If local disk is too small, the job fails with disk full errors during the shuffle phase.
[1x] Job fails mid-execution after hours of compute. Increasing executor memory or shuffle disk size is required. Hard to predict without profiling actual data sizes.
[1x] Shuffle / Spill to Disk Overflow
[1x] Batch job re-runs after a failure but appends to output instead of overwriting or using a unique run-id partition. The same records are written twice into the destination table or file.
[1x] Duplicate data in analytics tables. Downstream counts and aggregations are inflated. Requires full reprocessing to clean up.
[1x] Idempotency Broken on Re-run
[1x] A downstream batch job starts before its upstream dependency has finished writing. Reads a partial dataset because orchestration dependency is misconfigured (missing wait condition or incorrect schedule offset).
[1x] Batch operates on incomplete input data. Output appears valid but is missing a portion of records. Extremely hard to detect without row-count validation.
[1x] Dependency Job Not Complete
[1x] Batch job runs far longer than expected (due to data skew, hot partitions, or under-provisioned workers) on a pay-per-second cloud resource. No cost guardrails or max-duration limits are set.
[1x] Unexpectedly high compute bill. Common on on-demand or serverless batch (AWS Glue, Dataproc). Add job duration alerts and auto-termination policies.
[1x] Cost Overrun on Long-Running Job
[1x] Third-party API becomes slow. Threads waiting for the response accumulate, exhausting the thread pool. Your service becomes unavailable not due to internal issues but due to an external dependency stall.
[1x] Your entire service becomes unresponsive due to an external dependency. Requires circuit breaker + bulkhead isolation.
[1x] External API Timeout Cascade
[1x] Your service exceeds the API quota of an external provider. Often due to a spike in user activity, a batch operation calling the external API in a tight loop, or missing rate-limit-aware client logic.
[1x] External calls fail; your service must queue, backoff, and retry \u2014 or degrade gracefully. No quick fix without quota increase.
[1x] External Rate Limit Hit (429)
[1x] Circuit breaker detects sustained failures from an external dependency and opens the circuit, fast-failing all subsequent calls without attempting them. State is open until half-open probe succeeds.
[1x] All requests to the downstream service fail immediately. Requires a fallback path or user-facing degraded state.
[1x] Your service cannot deliver a webhook to a partner because their endpoint is down, returning non-2xx, or timing out. Retry with exponential backoff; if all retries exhaust, the event is lost or DLQ'd.
[1x] Partner does not receive events; data sync breaks silently. Partner may call with inconsistent state complaints days later.
[1x] Webhook Delivery Failure
[1x] External hostname cannot be resolved \u2014 DNS server unreachable, NXDOMAIN for a decommissioned service, or TTL-expired record not yet refreshed. Causes immediate connection failure.
[1x] All calls to the external service fail with a socket error rather than an HTTP error. Harder to diagnose without DNS-level observability.
[1x] The external API's certificate has expired, its hostname doesn't match, or it presents an untrusted CA. Your HTTP client refuses to connect if strict TLS validation is enabled.
[1x] All calls to the external service fail with TLS errors. May require disabling strict validation as a workaround \u2014 creates security risk.
[1x] TLS Certificate Error on Upstream
[1x] External API goes down. All clients retry simultaneously without jitter. The thundering herd of retries hammers the recovering external service, delaying its recovery.
[1x] External service stays down longer due to your own retry traffic. Exponential backoff + full jitter is required.
[1x] Retry Storm on External Failure
[1x] External API silently changes its response shape \u2014 renamed field, changed type, dropped required key. Deserialization in your code throws an exception or produces null values.
[1x] Integration breaks silently or with serialization errors. No SLA guarantee from the provider. Requires defensive deserialization.
[1x] Response Schema Change (Breaking)
[1x] OAuth token, API key, or service account credential has expired or been rotated by the external provider. All calls fail with 401 Unauthorized until credential is refreshed and redeployed.
[1x] Hard-stop failure for all operations requiring that external service. Requires credential rotation procedure and automation.
[1x] Credentials / API Key Expired
[1x] Calling a mutating external API (payment, order creation) without an idempotency key. On retry after a network failure, the operation is executed twice \u2014 charging the customer twice.
[1x] Financial or data integrity issues. Extremely difficult to detect without reconciliation. Requires idempotency key on all mutating calls.
[1x] Idempotency Key Missing
[1x] HTTP client connection pool to external service is too small. Under load, requests queue waiting for a connection slot. Adds latency proportional to queue wait time.
[1x] Downstream latency bleeds into your response time. Looks like your service is slow when the bottleneck is the connection pool size.
[1x] Connection Pool Starvation (Downstream)
[1x] Inbound webhook from external provider fails HMAC/signature verification \u2014 either due to a clock skew beyond tolerance, key mismatch after rotation, or a replay attack being blocked.
[1x] Legitimate webhooks rejected; partner events missed. Clock sync and key rotation procedures must be rock-solid.
[1x] Webhook Signature Verification Fail
[1x] External API responses cached aggressively to reduce quota usage. External data changes but your cache returns the stale version past its usefulness, causing incorrect behaviour.
[1x] Users see stale external data. Requires cache invalidation strategy aligned with data freshness requirements.
[1x] Response Caching Staleness
[1x] Integration silently breaks. May go undetected if the 410 response has a plausible-looking body. Monitor all non-2xx responses from external APIs.
[1x] Vendor API Deprecation (Silent 410)
[1x] Multiple concurrent threads detect an expired OAuth token and all simultaneously attempt to refresh it. Each thread obtains a different new token; earlier tokens are invalidated by the last refresh, causing 401s on concurrent requests.
[1x] Burst of 401 errors under high concurrency. Requires a single-flight token refresh with a lock so only one goroutine/thread refreshes at a time.
[1x] OAuth Token Race Condition
[1x] External integration is expected to deliver data or events within a contractual time window (e.g., payment settlements, order confirmations). A compute or network issue causes the delivery to fall outside the window.
[1x] SLA breach with financial or legal consequences. Requires latency alerting on integration pipelines, not just error-rate alerting.
[1x] Partner SLA Window Missed
[1x] External API responds with a 301/302 that redirects back to itself or into a cycle. HTTP client follows up to its redirect limit (e.g., 10) then throws an error, wasting time and network round-trips.
[1x] Each call wastes 10\xd7 the network round-trips before failing. High latency and connection count on calls to that endpoint.
[1x] Redirect Loop on External Endpoint
[1x] Your compute runs in a private subnet with restrictive egress rules. A newly added external API domain or IP range is not whitelisted. Calls fail at the network layer with ECONNREFUSED or timeout, not an HTTP error.
[1x] Integration fails silently with a network error that looks like an outage on the external side. Requires egress firewall audit and domain-based allow-listing.
[1x] Proxy / Egress Firewall Block
[1x] Integration relies on polling an external API for updates at a fixed interval. Events occur faster than the polling interval, causing some events to be missed between polls or overwritten by newer state.
[1x] Events silently dropped between poll cycles. Requires switching to webhook-based push delivery or shortening the poll interval with proper backoff.
[1x] Polling Inefficiency (Missed Events)
[1x] Kubernetes node stops reporting to the control plane \u2014 kubelet crash, network partition, or host OS freeze. All pods on the node are evicted after the node timeout and rescheduled elsewhere.
[1x] Any running profile loses all pods on that node simultaneously. Increases load on remaining nodes during reschedule window.
[1x] Container registry unreachable, auth token expired, or the image tag does not exist. Pods stay in ImagePullBackOff or ErrImagePull state \u2014 new or restarting pods cannot start.
[1x] Scale-out events and restarts fail silently. Traffic continues routing to existing healthy pods until they run out.
[1x] Node runs low on memory or disk. Kubelet evicts lower-priority pods to free resources for higher-priority ones. Pods without PodDisruptionBudget protection are evicted without warning.
[1x] Low-priority profiles (Batch, Background Jobs) evicted first. High-priority (Sync API) pods may still be affected if eviction is severe enough.
[1x] Pod Eviction (Resource Pressure)
[1x] Pod references a ConfigMap or Secret that doesn't exist or was deleted. Pod fails to start or crashes on first read of the missing configuration at runtime.
[1x] All profiles dependent on that config fail to start. New deployments and pod restarts fail until the missing resource is restored.
[1x] ConfigMap / Secret Not Found
[1x] HPA takes 1\u20133 minutes to detect high CPU/memory and provision new pods. Traffic spike hits before scale-out completes, overloading existing pods during the scale lag window.
[1x] Brief overload period on every traffic spike. High-priority profiles suffer most; batch/background profiles tolerate it better.
[1x] Horizontal Autoscaler Lag
[1x] Persistent Volume Claim cannot be mounted \u2014 storage backend unavailable, PVC stuck in Terminating, or wrong access mode (ReadWriteOnce mounted by multiple pods in different nodes).
[1x] Any profile writing to persistent storage (logs, checkpoints, uploads) fails to start or crashes at first write.
[1x] PVC / Storage Mount Failure
[1x] CPU/memory requests set too low relative to actual usage. Pod scheduled on a node without enough headroom; throttled immediately under load. Limits set too high; node over-provisioned.
[1x] Scheduling issues, CPU throttling, or OOM kills depending on which side is misconfigured. Affects all profiles but severity depends on workload type.
[1x] Resource Request/Limit Mismatch
[1x] No PDB configured. During cluster upgrade or node drain, Kubernetes may simultaneously evict all pods of a deployment, causing full downtime instead of rolling disruption.
[1x] Any maintenance window or upgrade can cause zero-instance availability for any profile. PDB with minAvailable=1 prevents this.
[1x] Missing PodDisruptionBudget
[1x] Kubernetes etcd is overloaded by excessive API calls (controller loops, too many watchers, large CRD objects). API server becomes slow or unavailable; scheduler and controller-manager stop working.
[1x] New pods cannot be scheduled; deployments hang; HPA stops scaling. Existing running pods are unaffected but no changes can be applied until etcd recovers.
[1x] etcd Overload / Control Plane Lag
[1x] A new or modified Kubernetes NetworkPolicy inadvertently blocks traffic between namespaces, or between a service and its dependencies. Traffic that previously worked silently drops after the policy is applied.
[1x] Service-to-service calls fail with connection refused or timeout. All profiles relying on internal service mesh communication are affected. Policy audit required.
[1x] Network Policy Blocking Internal Traffic
[1x] Pod's Kubernetes service account lacks the RBAC permission or IAM role binding (IRSA on EKS, Workload Identity on GKE) to access a cloud resource. Calls to S3, Secrets Manager, or other cloud APIs fail with 403.
[1x] Any profile accessing cloud services (reading secrets, writing to S3, calling Bedrock/OpenAI, etc.) fails silently or loudly with access denied errors.
[1x] Service Account / IRSA Permission Error
[1x] CoreDNS pods are overloaded by excessive DNS lookups from the cluster (e.g., every HTTP call triggers a fresh DNS lookup). CoreDNS becomes a bottleneck; lookups queue up and time out.
[1x] Service discovery breaks intermittently. Calls to internal and external services fail with DNS timeout errors. Mitigated by enabling ndots tuning and in-pod DNS caching.
[1x] DNS Cache Poisoning / CoreDNS Overload
[1x] Application emitting extremely high log volumes saturates the log shipper (Fluentd, Fluent Bit). Log buffer fills; new log entries are dropped or the shipper applies backpressure, causing the application's write to /dev/log to block.
[1x] Logs are silently dropped during high-traffic periods \u2014 exactly when you need them most for debugging. In extreme cases, application throughput degrades due to blocked log writes.
[1x] Log Volume / Fluentd Backpressure
